والتعبير برأس الأربعين يفيد أنه بعثَ عند استكمالها من غير زيادة ولا نقص وهو الصحيح الذي عليه الجمهور (۲)، ولكن هذا لا يتم إلا لو كانت البعثة في شهر الولادة، مع أن المشهور أنه ولد في ربيع الأول وبُعثَ في رمضان، فله حين البعث أربعون سنة ونصف سنة، إن كان البعث في رمضان الواقع بعد السنة المتممة للأربعين أو تسع وثلاثون ونصف إن كان البعث في رمضان الواقع في أثناء السنة المتممة للأربعين فمن قال أربعون سنة، ألغى الكسْرَ على الأول، أو جبرة على الثاني. وقال بعضهم : كان ابتداء الوحي بالمنام في ربيع ومكث ستة أشهر كذلك، ومن قال: كان ابتداؤُهُ في رمضان أراد مجية جبريل يقظةً ; فرجع الخلافُ لفظيًّا ولا كسر. والصحيح أن نبوته ورسالته مقترنتان، وقال ابن عبد البر وغيره : أرسله الله لما ثلاثاً وأربعين سنةً، فكانت النبوة سابقةً بنزول (اقْرَأْ)، وكانت الرسالة بأمره بالإنذار لما نزلت آية (المدَّثر)، فهو في زمن فترة الوحي نبي لا رسول. وأجاب القائلون بالأول بأن آية (المدثر) بيان للمراد من سورة (اقْرَأْ) لأن المعنى: اقرأ على قومك ما سنبينه لك، وإنما كان الإرسال على رأس الأربعين لأنه العادة المستمرة في معظم الأنبياء أو جميعهم كما جزم به - :أي بالثاني) - كثيرون، منهم شيخ الإسلام في حواشي البيضاوي (٥) ، وإنما استدلُّوا بالعادة المستمرة، ولم يستدلوا بحديث: ما من نبي نبئ إلا على رأس الأربعين سنة، لعد ابن الجوزي له (۱) في الموضوعات (۱) ، وذكر العلامة الشيخ الأمير (۳) والعلامة الشيخ الشنواني: أن الحقِّ أن هذا السنَّ غالب فقط في النبوة، وإلا فقد نبي عيسى ورفع إلى السماء قبله وكان عمره ثلاثاً وثلاثين سنة، ونبي يحيى صبيا بناءً على أن الحكم الذي أُوتِيَهُ صبيا : النبوة . انتهى . ولكن ذكروا في حواشي التفسير نقلاً عن المواهب أن هذا خلاف التحقيق (۳) ، وقالوا : الصحيح أن عيسى ما رفع إلا بعد مضي ثمانين سنة من النبوة وبعد نزوله من السماء يعيش أربعين سنة، ولا يرد قوله تعالى في حقّ يحيى : ﴿وَآتَيْنَاهُ الْحُكْمَ صَبِيًّا﴾ [مريم: ١٢] لأن المراد بالحكم العلم والمعرفة لا النبوة ولا يرد أيضاً قوله تعالى حكاية عن عيسى : ﴿آتَانِيَ الْكِتَابَ وَجَعَلَنِي نَبِيًّا﴾ [مريم: ٣٠] ; لأنه من التعبير بالماضي عن المستقبل على حد قوله تعالى: ﴿أَتَى أَمْرُ اللهِ﴾ [النحل: ١] ، أو المعنى : وجعلني نبيا في علمه . هذا، ووقع في كلام سيدي علي الخواص: أن النبيَّ نُبي من صغره، ولعله أراد الكمال والتهيُّؤَ كما ذكره العلامة الأمير (٤)، والله أعلم بالحقيقة.
The expression 'at the head of forty' indicates that he was sent at its completion without increase or decrease, and that is the correct view held by the majority (2). However, this is only complete if the sending was in the month of birth, whereas the well-known is that he was born in Rabi' al-Awwal and sent in Ramadan. So at the time of the sending he was forty years and a half year, if the sending was in Ramadan that occurs after the year completing forty, or thirty-nine and a half if the sending was in Ramadan that occurs during the year completing forty. So whoever said forty years ignored the fraction on the first, or made it complete on the second. Some said: The beginning of revelation was in a dream in Rabi' and he remained six months likewise. And whoever said its beginning was in Ramadan meant the coming of Jibril in wakefulness, so the disagreement becomes verbal and there is no fraction. The correct view is that his prophethood and messengerhood are concurrent. Ibn 'Abd al-Barr and others said: Allah sent him when he was forty-three years; so prophethood preceded by the revelation of 'Iqra', and messengerhood came by his command to warn when the verse of al-Muddaththir was revealed, so during the interval of revelation he was a prophet not a messenger. The proponents of the first view answered that the verse of al-Muddaththir is an explanation of the intended meaning of Surat Iqra', because the meaning is: 'Recite to your people what We will clarify to you.' And the sending was only at the head of forty because it is the continuous custom in most prophets or all of them, as many asserted (including Shaykh al-Islam in his glosses on al-Baydawi (5)). They argued by the continuous custom, and did not argue by the hadith: 'No prophet is sent except at the head of forty years,' because Ibn al-Jawzi counted it among the fabricated. (1) in al-Mawdu'at (1). And the scholar al-Shaykh al-Amir (3) and the scholar al-Shaykh al-Shanwani mentioned: The truth is that this age is merely predominant in prophethood, otherwise there was the prophet 'Isa, who was raised to heaven before that and his age was thirty-three years, and the prophet Yahya as a child, based on the view that the judgment given to him as a child is prophethood. End. But they mentioned in the glosses on the Tafsir, quoting from al-Mawahib, that this contradicts verification (3), and they said: The correct view is that 'Isa was not raised except after eighty years of prophethood, and after his descent from heaven he will live forty years. And the statement of Allah Almighty regarding Yahya: 'And We gave him judgment as a child' (19:12) is not a counterargument, because by 'judgment' is meant knowledge and understanding, not prophethood. Nor is the statement of Allah Almighty quoted from 'Isa: 'He gave me the Book and made me a prophet' (19:30) a counterargument, because it is an expression of the past for the future, like His statement: 'The command of Allah has come' (16:1). Or the meaning is: 'He made me a prophet in His knowledge.' And there occurred in the speech of Sayyidi 'Ali al-Khawwass: 'The prophet was given prophethood from his childhood,' but perhaps he meant preparation and readiness, as mentioned by al-Amir (4). And Allah knows best the truth.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the Prophet Muhammad's exact age at the onset of prophethood, focusing on the expression 'at the head of forty' found in the poem. The author first states the majority opinion that the Prophet was sent at the completion of forty years, with no increase or decrease. However, he immediately notes a problem: the sending took place in Ramadan, while birth was in Rabi' al-Awwal, resulting in a half-year discrepancy. He presents possible calculations: if the sending Ramadan occurs after the fortieth year, the age is 40½; if during the fortieth year, 39½. Those who say exactly forty either ignore the fraction (on the first scenario) or round up (on the second). Some scholars resolve this by distinguishing between the initial dream revelation (in Rabi') and the waking revelation of Jibril (in Ramadan), making the disagreement merely verbal. The author then reports the view of Ibn 'Abd al-Barr that the Prophet was forty-three at sending, separating prophethood (with 'Iqra') from messengerhood (with al-Muddaththir). The proponents of the forty-year view respond that al-Muddaththir clarifies the meaning of 'Iqra'. They rely on the continuous custom (al-ada al-mustamirra) among most or all prophets, not on a specific hadith which was deemed weak or fabricated by Ibn al-Jawzi. The discussion then addresses an objection: if forty is the custom, how about 'Isa (raised at 33) and Yahya (given judgment as a child)? The author reports two responses: (1) Some say forty is merely predominant (ghalib), not absolute. (2) Others, citing al-Mawahib, assert that 'Isa's raising was after 80 years of prophethood, and he will live 40 years after descent, removing the counterexample. For Yahya, 'judgment' means knowledge, not prophethood. For 'Isa's statement 'He made me a prophet' is a past tense for future (like 'The command of Allah has come'). Finally, the author notes a statement by 'Ali al-Khawwass that the Prophet was given prophethood from childhood, but interprets it as preparedness. The passage concludes with 'Allah knows best.' The author presents these views without fully endorsing one, showing scholarly caution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet was exactly forty years old at the moment of prophethood with no discrepancy.
Correction: The passage shows that based on birth in Rabi' al-Awwal and sending in Ramadan, there is a half-year difference, leading scholars to either ignore the fraction, round up, or distinguish between dream and waking revelation.

Checkpoint
What main argument did the proponents of the forty-year view rely on, according to the passage?
A) A specific hadith about the age of forty.
B) The continuous custom among most or all prophets.
C) The consensus of all scholars.
D) The explicit statement of the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
The exact age of the Prophet at prophethood is a matter of scholarly debate, with the majority view of exactly forty years defended by the continuous custom and a distinction between dream and waking revelation, while alternative views and objections are carefully addressed.

Resolving the Apparent Delay in Jihad's Legislation
The commentator addresses the apparent contradiction between the verse's implication of immediate jihad and its historical delay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars resolve the tension between the grammatical implication of immediate jihad in the poem and its historical postponement until after the Hijra.

Core Idea
The grammatical immediacy of the fa' does not always demand chronological immediacy.

In the poem, the phrase 'fa-arshada' seems to say the Prophet guided by the sword immediately after being sent. Yet history shows jihad was legislated later. The commentator takes up this problem, showing how careful attention to language and context resolves the difficulty. This lesson guides you through the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradiction
Read the passage and note the grammatical implication of the fa' versus the historical fact. The fa' suggests immediate jihad, but history records a delay.
2
Consider the proposed solution and its objection
Look at the suggestion that immediacy is relative. Then see why this solution is objected: the analogy with 'married then had a child' doesn't apply because jihad could have been earlier.
3
Examine the alternative answer
Read the alternative answer that jihad was not possible before that time. Think about what might have made jihad possible later (e.g., establishment of a state).
The passage cuts off; you may infer that the answer refers to the need for a stable community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (فَأَرْشَدَ ...) إلى آخره : معطوف على قوله : (جاء بالتوحيد) فيقتضي أن النبي أرشد الخلق بالسيف عقب الإرسال ; لأن الفاء تقتضي التعقيب، مع أن الجهاد لم يشرع بفور الإرسال، بل بعد الهجرة بسنة; لأنه شرع في صفر من السنة الثانية من الهجرة كما نبه عليه الحلبي في «السيرة» (۲) ، وقد يقال : التعقيب في كلِّ شيء بحسبه، ونُوقش في ذلك بأنه لا يقال ذلك إلا إذا كان المذكور لا يمكن وجوده قبل مضي المدة التي بينه وبين المعطوف عليه كما في : تزوّج زيد فولد له، وهنا الجهاد يمكن حصوله قبل هذه المدَّة، وأجابَ بعضُهم بأن الجهاد غير ممكن قبل هذه
His statement 'fa-arshada...' up to its end: it is co-ordinated to his statement 'jā'a bi-l-tawḥīd' and it requires that the Prophet guided the creation by the sword immediately after the sending, because the fā' indicates immediate consequence, even though jihad was not legislated immediately upon the sending, but rather one year after the Hijra; because it was legislated in Safar of the second year of the Hijra as al-Halabī pointed out in al-Sīra. And it may be said: immediate consequence is relative to each thing. And that was objected to by saying that such an expression is only used when the thing mentioned cannot exist before the passage of the time between it and that which it is co-ordinated to, as in 'Zayd married then he had a child', and here jihad could have occurred before this time. And some answered that jihad is not possible before this time...
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage discusses a problem of historical chronology relative to the poetic verse. The verse says that the Prophet guided people by the sword immediately (fa- indicates immediate sequence). But historically jihad was legislated only after the Hijra, not immediately upon prophethood. The commentator considers possible answers: first, that immediate sequence is relative to context. This is objected to because the analogy fails; jihad could have been earlier. Then a possible answer is that jihad was not possible before that time (the end of the sentence is missing in our text).

Correction: The fa' can indicate logical or relative sequence, not necessarily chronological immediacy; historical evidence shows jihad was legislated later.

The Real and Metaphorical Meaning of Irshad and Its Universality
Author explains irshad (guidance) as real or metaphorical and its universal scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of irshad (real vs metaphorical) and how the Prophet's guidance is universal over all creation, including those not met in person.

Core Idea
Guidance has two meanings: making guided (specific) and indicating (universal).

The author begins by clarifying the term 'irshad' (guidance) as used in the poem. He explains that it can be understood in two ways: as making someone actually guided, or as simply showing the way. This distinction has important implications for who is included in the Prophet's guidance. The passage then expands on the scope of 'the creation' and addresses a potential objection about those who never met the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two meanings of irshad
Read the first part where irshad is defined as real (making guided) or metaphorical (indication). Notice the consequence for who is included.
Real guidance is only for believers; metaphorical guidance is for all.
2
Identify the scope of 'creation'
The author explains that creation includes humans, jinn, and by some views angels and animals. Note the difference between mission of obligation and honor.
Angels and animals are included but as a mission of honor, not obligation.
3
Examine the objection and answer
Read the question about those not met in person and the answer that guidance can be through intermediaries. The hadith supports this.
The universality is preserved through indirect conveyance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعنى الإرشاد الحقيقي تصييرُهُم راشدين؛ أي: مهديين. وفسروه مجازاً بالدلالة، فإن حُمِلَ على الأول، كان خاصا بمن آمن وإن حُملَ على الثاني كان عاماً لمن آمن ولمن كفر . وقوله : (الخَلْقَ) أي : جميع الثقلين الإنس والجن إجماعاً، وكذا الملائكة بناءً على أنه مرسل إليهم إرسال تكليف والراجح أنه مرسل إليهم إرسال تشريف، كما تقدَّم لك تحريرُهُ، وإن رجح بعضهم هنا خلافه، وأما إرساله إلى سائر الحيوانات، فإرسال تشريف قطعاً. فإن قلت: كيف يستقيم العموم في الخلق مع أنه لو لم يرشد من لم يجتمع به؟ قلت : الإرشاد أعم من أن يكون بنفسه كمن اجتمع به، أو بواسطة كمن جاء بعده أو كان في زمنه ولم يجتمع به، وقد قال : يبلغ الشاهد منكم الغائب، فربَّ مبلغ أوعى مِنْ سامع
The meaning of real guidance is making them guided; i.e., rightly directed. And they interpreted it metaphorically as indication. If it is taken as the first, it is specific to those who believed; if taken as the second, it is general for those who believed and those who disbelieved. And His saying: 'the creation' i.e., all the two weighty ones, humans and jinn, by consensus, and likewise the angels, based on the view that he was sent to them as a mission of obligation, and the more correct view is that he was sent to them as a mission of honor, as you have previously had its verification, although some here have preferred the opposite. As for his sending to the rest of the animals, it is a mission of honor absolutely. If you say: How is the generality over creation sound, considering that he did not guide those he did not meet? I say: Guidance is more general than being in person, like those he met, or through an intermediary, like those who came after him or were in his time but did not meet him. And he said: 'Let the one present convey to the absent, for perhaps one who is conveyed to is more perceptive than one who hears.'
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage explains the word 'irshad' (guidance) as used in the poem. The author states that 'real guidance' means making people guided (rāshidīn), i.e., rightly directed. However, scholars have also interpreted it metaphorically as 'indication' or 'showing the way' (dalāla). The author then explains the consequence of these two interpretations: if 'irshad' is taken in its real sense (making people guided), then it applies specifically to those who actually believed, because only believers become truly guided. If it is taken metaphorically as indication, then it applies generally to both believers and disbelievers, since the Prophet showed the way to all. Next, the author discusses the object of guidance: 'the creation' (al-khalq). He explains that this includes all of the two weighty ones (al-thaqalayn): humans and jinn, by consensus. As for angels, there is a difference: some say the Prophet was sent to them as a mission of obligation (irsāl taklīf), but the preponderant view is that it was a mission of honor (irsāl tashrīf), which the author mentions has been discussed previously. However, some scholars here have preferred the opposite. As for animals, the mission is absolutely one of honor, not obligation, because they lack the capacity for rational obligation. The author then raises a potential objection: How can the Prophet's guidance be universal if he did not meet everyone? The answer is that guidance is broader than personal encounter; it can be through intermediaries (like those who convey his message after him) or through those who lived in his time but did not meet him. The author supports this with the Prophet's hadith: 'Let the one present convey to the absent, for perhaps one who is conveyed to is more perceptive than one who hears.' Thus, the universality is maintained.

دلالة
الخلق

إرسال تكليف
إرسال تشريف

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Prophet only guided those he met in person.
Correction: But the author shows guidance can be through intermediaries, as per the hadith: 'Let the one present convey to the absent.'

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's guidance is universal, either in the real sense for believers or the metaphorical sense for all, and it extends to all creation, even those not met, through intermediaries.

The Interpretation of 'Bi-Sayfihi' in the Poem
Author analyzes the phrase 'bi-sayfihi' with two grammatical interpretations and identifies a metonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible grammatical interpretations of 'bi-sayfihi' in the line, and how the poet uses metonymy (majāz mursal) to mean the instrument of jihad.

Core Idea
The sword in the poem is a symbol of jihad, not a physical tool of guidance.

This passage delves into the precise meaning of the phrase 'with his sword' (bi-sayfihi) from the poem. The author explores two grammatical possibilities and explains the metaphorical extension. Understanding this helps us see how the poet conveys that the Prophet's guidance is accompanied by the struggle of jihad.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once for flow. Notice the grammatical terms 'حال' and 'باء تعدية'.
The text is dense with grammatical analysis.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The author gives two scenarios depending on the meaning of 'arshad'. Find each one and note the role of the bā' preposition.
3
Understand the metaphor
The author explains that 'sword' is used metonymically for the instrument of jihad, including even stones. Ask: why is this significant?
This connects to the broader theme of jihad in the poem.
4
Reflect on the core idea
Summarize why the sword is not the literal means of guidance but a symbol of the broader struggle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بِسَيْفِهِ) يحتمل أن يكونَ متعلقاً بحال محذوفة من فاعل (أرشد) أي: أرشد الخلق لدين الحقِّ في حال كونه ملتبساً بسيفه أو حال كونه مُلْجِنَّاً لهم بسيفه ; لأن الإرشاد والدلالة ليسا بالسيف حتى تكون الباء للتعدية بل باللسان قطعاً، وهذا إذا جعل (أرشد) بمعنى دلَّ، أما إذا جعل بمعنى صيَّرهم راشدين على أن المراد أمَّةُ الإجابة، فالباء للسببية، وإضافة سيف للضمير لأدنى ملابسة; لأن المراد بالسيف: السيف الذي جاءَ بمشروعية مقاتلة أعداء الله به، سواء كان بيده أو بيد من تبعه ولو إلى يوم القيامة، والمراد بالسيف آلة الجهاد التي يباح قتال الحربيين بها، حتى الحجارة، فقد رمى بالحجر في يوم ففي كلام المصنف مجاز مرسل ; من إطلاق الخاص وإرادة العام فهو من باب عموم المجاز ; أي: المجاز العام الشامل للحقيقة والمجاز . وقد كان له صلى الله عليه وسلم سيوف متعدّدة ; منها المأثورُ ; وهو أوَّلُ سيف ملكه ; لأنه ورثَهُ من أبيه، ومنها القضيب بالقاف والضاد، ومنها ذو الفقار بفتح الفاء وكسرها , ومنها غير ذلك، وقد دفع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم للعكاشة جزل حطب حين انكسر سيفه يوم بدر وقال : اضرب بهِ»، فعادَ في يدِهِ سيفاً صارماً طويلاً أبيض شديد المتن، فقاتل به. قوله : (وَهَدْيِهِ لِلحَقِّ) عطفٌ على (سيفه)، فيصير التقدير وأرشدَهُم بهديه للحقِّ، لكن يلزم عليه تهافت ; إذ التقدير ودلَّهُم بدلالته إلا أن تُجعل الباء للتصوير، فتحصل أن الباء من حيث دخولها على السيف للملابسة أو للسببية كما تقدَّم بيانه
His saying: 'with his sword (bi-sayfihi)' can be attached to a deleted circumstantial accusative from the agent of 'arshad', i.e., 'He guided the creation to the religion of truth while being in the state of being clothed with his sword or while being in the state of making them take refuge with his sword'. Because guidance and indication are not by the sword such that the bā' is for transitivity, but rather by the tongue absolutely. This is if 'arshad' is taken in the meaning of 'indicated'. But if it is taken in the meaning of 'He made them guided', intending the nation of response, then the bā' is for causality, and the addition of 'sword' to the pronoun is for the slightest connection, because what is meant by 'sword' is the sword that came with the legislation of fighting against the enemies of Allah with it, whether it be in his hand or in the hand of those who followed him until the Day of Judgment. And what is meant by 'sword' is the instrument of jihad by which fighting against combatants is permitted, even stones, for he threw with a stone on a day. So in the author's words there is a metonymy (majāz mursal) from the particular to the general; it is of the type of general metaphor, i.e., the general metaphor that includes the literal and the figurative. And he (peace be upon him) had multiple swords: among them was al-Ma'thūr, which was the first sword he owned because he inherited it from his father; and among them was al-Qadīb (with qāf and ḍād); and among them was Dhū al-Fiqār (with fatḥ on the fā' and kasr on it); and others. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave to ‘Ukāsha a chunk of wood when his sword broke on the day of Badr and said: 'Strike with it', and it became in his hand a sharp, long, white, strong-bladed sword, and he fought with it. His saying: 'and his guidance to the truth (wa-hadyihi lil-ḥaqq)' is conjoined to 'his sword', so the تقدير becomes 'and he guided them with his guidance to the truth', but it entails a contradiction because the تقدير would be 'and he indicated them with his indication' unless the bā' is made for taswīr (depiction). Thus it is established that the bā', regarding its entry on the sword, is for connection or for causality, as previously explained.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological analysis of the phrase 'bi-sayfihi' (with his sword) from the poem. The author first offers two possible interpretations of the prepositional phrase: it could be an adverbial of state (ḥāl) from the agent of 'arshad', meaning the Prophet guided while being armed with his sword, or while making the people take refuge with his sword. However, guidance and indication are not done by the sword (violence) but by speech, so the bā' cannot be for transitivity (ta‘diya) if 'arshad' means 'indicated'. If 'arshad' is taken in its real sense (making people guided), specifically for the 'nation of response', then the bā' is for causality (sababiyya): the sword was the cause of their becoming guided through jihad. The attachment of 'sword' to the pronoun is loose, meaning the sword of jihad legislation. The sword here is a metaphor (majāz mursal) for the instrument of jihad, including even stones, as the Prophet once threw a stone. It is a general metaphor encompassing both literal and figurative meanings. The author then digresses to list the Prophet's physical swords: al-Ma'thūr (inherited from his father), al-Qadīb, Dhū al-Fiqār, and others. He recounts the story of ‘Ukāsha: during the Battle of Badr, when his sword broke, the Prophet gave him a piece of wood which miraculously turned into a sharp sword. Finally, the author begins to discuss the next phrase 'wa-hadyihi lil-ḥaqq' (and his guidance to the truth), which is conjoined to 'sayfihi'. This would yield 'and he guided them with his guidance to the truth', but this seems tautological (guiding with guidance). The author notes this contradiction can be resolved by making the bā' for taswīr (depiction), i.e., the guidance is depicted as being like the sword. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating further discussion.

Key Terms
سيف
مجاز مرسل
باء تعدية
حال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the phrase 'with his sword' implies the Prophet directly used physical violence to guide people.
Correction: The author clarifies that guidance is by the tongue, not the sword. The sword is a metaphor for the legislation of jihad, and the bā' indicates causality, not instrumentality.

Checkpoint
When 'arshad' is taken in its real sense of making people guided, what is the function of the bā' in 'bi-sayfihi'?
A) Bā' of transitivity
B) Bā' of causality
C) Bā' of accompaniment
D) Bā' of instrument

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'with his sword' is a metonymy for the divine legislation of jihad, and its preposition can be interpreted as either circumstantial or causal depending on the meaning of 'arshad'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of 'guidance to the truth', do you naturally associate it with force or with persuasion? How does this lesson's distinction between 'guidance by the tongue' and 'guidance by the sword' challenge your own understanding of how people are brought to Islam?
?
The sword here represents the legislation of jihad, which involves struggle and sacrifice. In your own life, where are you tempted to 'use the sword'—to force your views on others—rather than using the tongue with wisdom and patience?
?
If the sword is a metonymy for any legitimate weapon of jihad, including stones, what does this teach you about the seriousness of armed struggle in Islam? Are you truly prepared to defend the deen with your life, or do you shy away from that responsibility?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بِسَيْفِهِ) يحتمل أن يكونَ متعلقاً بحال محذوفة من فاعل (أرشد) أي: أرشد الخلق لدين الحقِّ في حال كونه ملتبساً بسيفه أو حال كونه مُلْجِنَّاً لهم بسيفه ; لأن الإرشاد والدلالة ليسا بالسيف حتى تكون الباء للتعدية بل باللسان قطعاً، وهذا إذا جعل (أرشد) بمعنى دلَّ، أما إذا جعل بمعنى صيَّرهم راشدين على أن المراد أمَّةُ الإجابة، فالباء للسببية، وإضافة سيف للضمير لأدنى ملابسة; لأن المراد بالسيف: السيف الذي جاءَ بمشروعية مقاتلة أعداء الله به، سواء كان بيده أو بيد من تبعه ولو إلى يوم القيامة، والمراد بالسيف آلة الجهاد التي يباح قتال الحربيين بها، حتى الحجارة، فقد رمى بالحجر في يوم ففي كلام المصنف مجاز مرسل ; من إطلاق الخاص وإرادة العام فهو من باب عموم المجاز ; أي: المجاز العام الشامل للحقيقة والمجاز . وقد كان له صلى الله عليه وسلم سيوف متعدّدة ; منها المأثورُ ; وهو أوَّلُ سيف ملكه ; لأنه ورثَهُ من أبيه، ومنها القضيب بالقاف والضاد، ومنها ذو الفقار بفتح الفاء وكسرها , ومنها غير ذلك، وقد دفع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم للعكاشة جزل حطب حين انكسر سيفه يوم بدر وقال : اضرب بهِ»، فعادَ في يدِهِ سيفاً صارماً طويلاً أبيض شديد المتن، فقاتل به. قوله : (وَهَدْيِهِ لِلحَقِّ) عطفٌ على (سيفه)، فيصير التقدير وأرشدَهُم بهديه للحقِّ، لكن يلزم عليه تهافت ; إذ التقدير ودلَّهُم بدلالته إلا أن تُجعل الباء للتصوير، فتحصل أن الباء من حيث دخولها على السيف للملابسة أو للسببية كما تقدَّم بيانه
His saying: 'with his sword (bi-sayfihi)' can be attached to a deleted circumstantial accusative from the agent of 'arshad', i.e., 'He guided the creation to the religion of truth while being in the state of being clothed with his sword or while being in the state of making them take refuge with his sword'. Because guidance and indication are not by the sword such that the bā' is for transitivity, but rather by the tongue absolutely. This is if 'arshad' is taken in the meaning of 'indicated'. But if it is taken in the meaning of 'He made them guided', intending the nation of response, then the bā' is for causality, and the addition of 'sword' to the pronoun is for the slightest connection, because what is meant by 'sword' is the sword that came with the legislation of fighting against the enemies of Allah with it, whether it be in his hand or in the hand of those who followed him until the Day of Judgment. And what is meant by 'sword' is the instrument of jihad by which fighting against combatants is permitted, even stones, for he threw with a stone on a day. So in the author's words there is a metonymy (majāz mursal) from the particular to the general; it is of the type of general metaphor, i.e., the general metaphor that includes the literal and the figurative. And he (peace be upon him) had multiple swords: among them was al-Ma'thūr, which was the first sword he owned because he inherited it from his father; and among them was al-Qadīb (with qāf and ḍād); and among them was Dhū al-Fiqār (with fatḥ on the fā' and kasr on it); and others. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave to ‘Ukāsha a chunk of wood when his sword broke on the day of Badr and said: 'Strike with it', and it became in his hand a sharp, long, white, strong-bladed sword, and he fought with it. His saying: 'and his guidance to the truth (wa-hadyihi lil-ḥaqq)' is conjoined to 'his sword', so the تقدير becomes 'and he guided them with his guidance to the truth', but it entails a contradiction because the تقدير would be 'and he indicated them with his indication' unless the bā' is made for taswīr (depiction). Thus it is established that the bā', regarding its entry on the sword, is for connection or for causality, as previously explained.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological analysis of the phrase 'bi-sayfihi' (with his sword) from the poem. The author first offers two possible interpretations of the prepositional phrase: it could be an adverbial of state (ḥāl) from the agent of 'arshad', meaning the Prophet guided while being armed with his sword, or while making the people take refuge with his sword. However, guidance and indication are not done by the sword (violence) but by speech, so the bā' cannot be for transitivity (ta‘diya) if 'arshad' means 'indicated'. If 'arshad' is taken in its real sense (making people guided), specifically for the 'nation of response', then the bā' is for causality (sababiyya): the sword was the cause of their becoming guided through jihad. The attachment of 'sword' to the pronoun is loose, meaning the sword of jihad legislation. The sword here is a metaphor (majāz mursal) for the instrument of jihad, including even stones, as the Prophet once threw a stone. It is a general metaphor encompassing both literal and figurative meanings. The author then digresses to list the Prophet's physical swords: al-Ma'thūr (inherited from his father), al-Qadīb, Dhū al-Fiqār, and others. He recounts the story of ‘Ukāsha: during the Battle of Badr, when his sword broke, the Prophet gave him a piece of wood which miraculously turned into a sharp sword. Finally, the author begins to discuss the next phrase 'wa-hadyihi lil-ḥaqq' (and his guidance to the truth), which is conjoined to 'sayfihi'. This would yield 'and he guided them with his guidance to the truth', but this seems tautological (guiding with guidance). The author notes this contradiction can be resolved by making the bā' for taswīr (depiction), i.e., the guidance is depicted as being like the sword. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating further discussion.

Key Terms
سيف
قضيب
ذو الفقار
هَدْي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Prophet's physical swords themselves had intrinsic power or that the list of swords is the main point.
Correction: The swords are examples; the miraculous transformation of wood into a sword shows that Allah's support, not the material sword, is what matters. The list serves as background.

Checkpoint
Which sword did the Prophet inherit from his father?
A) Dhū al-Fiqār
B) Al-Qadīb
C) Al-Ma'thūr
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's swords are physical reminders of jihad, but the true miracle is from Allah, as shown by the transformation of wood into a sword for 'Ukasha.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where your 'sword' breaks—your strength, resource, or plan—do you trust that Allah can transform a mere piece of wood into a weapon of victory? What 'broken sword' in your life right now needs Allah's miraculous touch?
?
The Prophet's swords were physical reminders of Allah's guidance. What physical signs of guidance has Allah placed in your life (a verse, a friend, a trial) that you have overlooked or taken for granted?
?
The grammatical debate shows that even a single preposition (the bā') carries profound meaning. When you read the Qur'an, are you content with surface understanding, or do you dig into every word as if it contains a hidden treasure? What verse have you read casually that now demands deeper reflection?

The Attribute Al-Aqib: The Final Prophet
Commentator explains al-Aqib and defends finality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the attribute al-Aqib, its proof from hadith, and how the finality of prophethood does not conflict with the descent of 'Isa or the existence of Khidr and Ilyas.

Core Idea
The Prophet is the final prophet and the purpose of creation.

The commentator now explains the attribute al-Aqib, meaning 'the one who comes after.' This attribute affirms that Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) is the last prophet, and his law abrogates all previous ones. The passage also addresses potential objections by showing that the descent of 'Isa and the continued existence of Khidr and Ilyas do not contradict this finality, as they follow his law.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without stopping. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice how the passage moves from definition to proof to refutation.
2
Identify the key points
Identify three key parts: the meaning of al-Aqib, the hadith proof, and the refutation of objections. How does the commentator connect them?
The refutation specifically addresses 'Isa, Khidr, and Ilyas.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the Prophet is called the 'greatest fruit' and what that says about his role in creation.
The couplet at the end reinforces the idea that the final outcome is the goal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (العاقب) نعت لـ(محمد)، وهو الذي يأتي في العقب، وفسروه بأنه الذي تحشر الناس على قدمه؛ أي: على طريقه وشرعه؛ ففي الحديث: أنا العاقب فلا نبي بعدي؛ أي: تبتدأ نبوته فلا ينافي نزول عيسى في آخر الزمان، ووجود الخضر وإلياس الآن، وإنما كان الله هو العاقب ليكون شرعه ناسخاً لغيره من الشرائع، لا العكس ولأنه الثمرة العظمى؛ إذ هو المقصود من هذا العالم، والثمرة في الأشياء تأتي آخرها، وأنشدوا: نعم ما قال سادة الأول: أول الفكر آخر العمل.
His statement: (al-Aqib) is an attribute of (Muhammad), and it is the one who comes after. And they interpreted it as the one on whose step (i.e., path and law) the people will be gathered. So in the hadith: 'I am al-Aqib; there is no prophet after me.' That is, his prophethood begins, so it does not contradict the descent of Jesus at the end of time and the existence of Khidr and Elias now. And he was only made al-Aqib so that his law would be abrogating to other laws, not the opposite, and because he is the greatest fruit; for he is the purpose of this world, and fruit in things comes at their end. And they recited: How excellent is what the early masters said: The first of thought is the last of action.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage explains the attribute al-Aqib mentioned in the poem. Literally, it means 'the one who comes after.' The commentators interpret it as 'the one on whose path (method and law) people will be gathered.' The hadith states: 'I am al-Aqib; there is no prophet after me.' This refers to the fact that prophethood begins with him and ends with him. It clarifies that the descent of 'Isa at the end of time and the existence of Khidr and Ilyas (if they are still alive) do not contradict this finality, because the meaning is that no new prophet with a new law will come; rather, all will follow his law. The reason for his being the last is so that his law abrogates all previous laws, not the opposite. Additionally, he is likened to the greatest fruit: the purpose of creation, and fruit comes at the end. The passage concludes with a couplet: 'How excellent is what the early masters said: The first of thought is the last of action.' This illustrates the wisdom that the final outcome is the goal.

Key Terms
العاقب
عقب
ناسخ
ثمرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the presence of 'Isa or Khidr contradicts the finality of prophethood.
Correction: The passage explains that 'no prophet after me' means no new law; 'Isa and others will follow the Prophet's law.

Checkpoint
How does the passage respond to the objection that the descent of 'Isa and the existence of Khidr and Ilyas contradict the finality of prophethood?
A) It denies their existence.
B) It says they will follow the Prophet's law and that his law is abrogating, so their presence does not negate finality.
C) It says they are not prophets.
D) It says they will appear before him.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet is al-Aqib, the final prophet, and his law abrogates all others, making him the purpose of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet is the final and greatest fruit of creation, what fruit are you producing in your life that reflects his purpose? Are you living as if he is the ultimate purpose, or have you attached your heart to other goals?
?
The passage says his law abrogates all previous laws. What 'laws' or patterns from your pre-Islamic ignorance (jahiliyyah) or from other ideologies are you still holding onto? Will you let his sharia rule every corner of your life today?
?
You claim to believe there is no prophet after him—yet do you still look to modern 'guides' or 'reformers' for religious authority as if they can bring something new? What would it cost you to submit fully to the finality of his message?

Three Answers for Using 'Messengers' Instead of 'Prophets'
The commentator answers an objection with three explanations for why 'messengers' was used instead of 'prophets'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three scholarly explanations for why the author of the poem chose 'messengers' over 'prophets' when referring to the Prophet's finality.

Core Idea
Three answers show how precision in theological language reveals deeper meanings.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a subtle objection: if the Prophet is seal of both messengers and prophets, why does the poem only mention messengers? He provides three distinct answers, ranging from metaphorical interpretation to theological hierarchy. Each answer reflects a different scholarly approach to reconciling textual wording with doctrinal completeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
First, understand the objection that since the Prophet is seal of prophets as well, mentioning only messengers seems insufficient. The objector argues that being seal of messengers does not automatically imply being seal of prophets.
The objection is logical: 'seal of messengers' is a specific title that may not encompass prophets.
2
Examine the first two linguistic answers
The first answer says 'messengers' is used metaphorically to mean 'prophets'. The second answer proposes an ellipsis: the phrase implies 'messengers and prophets' just as a Quranic verse mentions only 'heat' to also mean 'cold'.
Both answers rely on Arabic rhetorical devices: majāz mursal and iktifā'.
3
Analyze the third theological answer and the debate
The third answer, from Shaykh al-Mallawī, claims that messenger and prophet are equal, but 'messengers' is more praiseworthy because risāla combines divine truth and creation. The commentator notes Ibn 'Abd al-Salām's opposing view and refutes it.
This answer elevates risāla over nubuwwa, contrary to some scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : كما أنه خاتم الرسل هو خاتم للأنبياء، فلم اقتصر المصنف على الأول مع أنه لا يلزم من ختمه للرسل ختمُهُ للأنبياء؟ أجيب بثلاثة أجوبة : الأول: أن المراد بالرسل الأنبياء، فقد أطلق الخاص وأراد العام مجازاً مرسلاً . الثاني : أن في الكلام اكتفاء، والتقدير : لرسل ربه وأنبيائه ؛ على حد قوله تعالى : سَرَابِيلَ تَقِيكُمُ الْحَرَّ [النحل: ۸۱] أي: والبرد. الثالث : ما قاله الشيخ الملوي من حمله على ما تقدم عن السعد من تساوي الرسول والنبي، وإنما اختار التعبير بالرسل لأنه أمدح ؛ فإن الرسالة أشرفُ من النبوة؛ لجمعها بين الحقِّ والخلق، خلافاً للعزّ بن عبد السلام في قوله بأن النبوة أفضلُ؛ معللاً بأن فيها الانصراف من حضرة الخلق إلى الحقِّ، والرسالة فيها الانصراف من حضرة الحقِّ إلى الخلق (۲) ، ورُدَّ بأن الرسالة فيها الجمع بينهما كما علمت
If it is said: just as he is the seal of the messengers, he is also the seal of the prophets, so why did the author restrict himself to the first (messengers) even though being seal of messengers does not necessitate being seal of prophets? He answered with three answers: First: that what is meant by 'messengers' is the prophets, so he used the specific and intended the general, by (the rhetorical figure called) mursal metaphor. Second: that in the speech there is conciseness, and the estimation is: 'to the messengers of his Lord and His prophets', analogous to the verse 'shirts that protect you from the heat' meaning 'and from the cold'. Third: what Shaykh al-Mallawī said, which is to carry it on what preceded from al-Saʿd of equality between messenger and prophet, and he only chose the expression 'messengers' because it is more praiseworthy; for the message (risāla) is more noble than prophecy (nubuwwa), because it combines between the right and creation, contrary to ʿIzz ibn ʿAbd al-Salām who says that prophecy is better, with the reasoning that in prophecy there is turning from the presence of creation to the Truth, and in the message there is turning from the presence of the Truth to the creation. And it was refuted that the message combines both, as you have learned.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and three answers. The objector argues that since the Prophet is also the seal of prophets, mentioning only 'messengers' in the poem is insufficient because being seal of messengers does not automatically make him seal of prophets. Three answers are given. 1) The term 'messengers' is used metaphorically (majāz mursal) to stand for 'prophets', i.e., using the specific term for the general. 2) The phrase is concise (iktifāʾ) and implies 'to the messengers and his prophets', similar to the Qur'anic expression 'shirts that protect you from the heat' which also implies protection from cold. 3) According to Shaykh al-Mallawī, drawing from al-Saʿd, 'messenger' and 'prophet' are equal, but the author chose 'messengers' because it is more laudable: risāla is superior to nubuwwa because it combines concern for the divine truth (ḥaqq) and engagement with creation (khalq). The commentator then mentions the opposing view of ʿIzz ibn ʿAbd al-Salām, who held nubuwwa as superior because it involves turning exclusively toward God, while risāla involves turning away to creation. The commentator refutes this by asserting that risāla actually combines both orientations, as the reader should understand.

Key Terms
مجاز مرسل
الاكتفاء
التقدير
الرسالة
النبوة
العز بن عبد السلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the three answers contradict each other, but they are alternative valid interpretations.
Correction: Each answer is a distinct scholarly approach; they are not mutually exclusive.

Checkpoint
According to the third answer, why did the author choose 'messengers' over 'prophets'?
A) Because messengers are a subset of prophets
B) Because 'messengers' is shorter
C) Because the message (risala) is more noble than prophecy (nubuwwa)
D) Because the verse requires it

Key Takeaway
The author's choice of 'messengers' can be justified through metaphorical language, ellipsis, or the superiority of risāla over nubuwwa.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the author's deliberate choice of 'messengers' over 'prophets', do you see your own tendency to use vague or imprecise language when describing Allah's messengers, and how might that diminish your reverence? What will you change today?
?
The third answer suggests that the messenger combines concern for both truth and creation. In your daily interactions, do you lean more toward retreating into personal worship (like the nubuwwa model) or actively engaging people with truth (like the risala model)? Which imbalance will you address this week?
?
This passage refutes Ibn Abd al-Salam's claim that prophecy is better because of exclusive focus on God. Are there areas in your life where you prioritize your own spiritual retreat over serving creation, thinking it is more 'noble'? How will you realign to the comprehensive path of the messenger?

Objection on Ahl al-Fatrah in Hell and Its Answer
Refutes objection from ahad hadiths with definitive Qur'an and special circumstances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars reconcile hadiths that seem to contradict the principle that ahl al-fatrah are not punished without a messenger.

Core Idea
Ahad hadiths cannot overrule definitive Qur'anic texts, and apparent exceptions may be due to hidden specifics.

The doctrine that ahl al-fatrah are not punished without a messenger is solidly based on the Qur'an. However, some hadiths mention that certain individuals from that period are in Hell. How do scholars reconcile this apparent contradiction? In this passage, the author presents two key arguments that preserve the general rule while accounting for the reported exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection begins with 'If it is said' (fa-in qila) followed by examples of individuals from ahl al-fatrah said to be in Hell.
This is a standard objection-answer format in kalam works.
2
Understand the first answer: ahad vs. qat'i
The author's first response is that the hadiths in question are ahad (non-mass transmitted) and cannot override the definitive Qur'anic verse. Focus on the principle that Qur'an takes precedence over weak hadiths.
The verse quoted is al-Isra 15: 'And We were not punishers until We had sent a messenger.'
3
Understand the second answer: special circumstances
The second response is that even if punishment is confirmed for some individuals, it may be due to a specific sin or condition known only to God and His messenger. This preserves the general rule.
This explains how the exceptions do not break the rule.
4
Reflect on the overall methodology
Consider how the author uses both textual hierarchy (qat'i vs. ahad) and contextual reasoning (special circumstances) to defend a theological principle. This is a typical method in Sunni kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: كيف هذا مع أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أخبر بأن جماعة من أهل الفترة في النار؛ كامرئ القيس وحاتم الطائي وبعض آباء الصحابة؛ فإن بعض الصحابة سأله وهو يخطب فقال: أين أبي؟ فقال: في النار؟ أجيب بأن أحاديثهم أحاديث آحاد وهي لا تعارض القطعي، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا كُنَّا مُعَذِّبِينَ حَتَّى نَبْعَثَ رَسُولا﴾ [الإسراء: ١٥]، وبأنه يجوز أن يكون تعذيب من صح تعذيبه منهم لأمر يختص به يعلمه الله تعالى ورسوله.
If it is said: How is this when the Prophet (peace be upon him) informed that a group of ahl al-fatrah are in the Fire, such as Imru' al-Qays, Hatim al-Ta'i, and some of the Companions' fathers? For some Companions asked him while he was delivering a sermon: 'Where is my father?' He said: 'In the Fire.' The answer is that their hadiths are ahad hadiths, and they do not conflict with the definitive (qat‘i), which is His statement: 'And We were not punishers until We had sent a messenger' [al-Isra: 15]. And also because it is possible that the punishment of those whose punishment is confirmed from them is due to something specific to them known only to God and His messenger.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This is an objection–answer (fa-in qīla ... ujība) passage. The objector raises a problem: the doctrine that ahl al-fatrah are saved seems contradicted by hadiths saying that specific individuals from that category (like Imru' al-Qays, Hatim al-Ta'i, and some fathers of Companions) are in Hellfire. The author provides two answers. First, these hadiths are ahad (transmitted by a few narrators) and cannot override definitive (qat‘i) texts, namely the Qur'anic verse 'And We were not punishers until We had sent a messenger' (Surah al-Isra, 15). Second, even if their punishment is established, it may be due to some special sin known only to God and His messenger, not due to general rule of ahl al-fatrah. Thus, the general principle remains intact.

Key Terms
أحاديث آحاد (ahad hadiths)
القطعي (al-qat‘i)
تعذيب (ta‘dhib)
رسول (rasool)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahad hadiths can universally contradict clear Qur'anic principles without possibility of reconciliation.
Correction: Ahad hadiths cannot override definitive (qat'i) texts, and any apparent conflict is reconciled by considering the hadith's weakness or by attributing the exception to special circumstances known to God.

Checkpoint
How does the author reconcile the hadiths that mention some ahl al-fatrah are in the Fire with the principle that ahl al-fatrah are saved?
A) He denies the authenticity of those hadiths
B) He says those hadiths are ahad and do not contradict the Qur'an, and the individuals may have been punished for specific reasons
C) He says that ahl al-fatrah are not actually saved
D) He says the hadiths are abrogated

Key Takeaway
Even when hadiths seem to contradict a clear Qur'anic principle, the conflict is resolved by noting the hadith's weak transmission or by attributing the exception to specific hidden causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a clear Qur'anic principle because of a single hadith you heard? What would it mean for your faith to always prioritize the Qur'an's definitive rulings over isolated reports?
?
When you hear about the fate of people who never received the message, does it move you to gratitude for your own guidance, or do you find yourself judging others? How does this passage challenge that attitude?
?
Think of a belief you hold about Allah's mercy and justice that might be based on weak evidence. Are you willing to re-examine it in light of the Qur'an's clear statements — even if it feels uncomfortable?

The Fate of Ahl al-Fatrah: Objection and Answer
The author presents an objection about ahl al-fatrah being in hell and gives two answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological objection concerning ahl al-fatrah being punished in Hell and the two responses that reconcile it with the definitive Quranic principle.

Core Idea
Solitary hadiths cannot overturn a definitive Quranic ruling, and individual exceptions may have hidden causes.

In this passage, the author addresses a difficult objection: how can some people who lived in the 'fatrah' (gap between prophets) be in Hell, when the Quran clearly states that no punishment occurs until a messenger is sent? By examining the nature of the evidence and allowing for hidden causes, the author defends the principle while acknowledging the prophetic reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and examples
Read the objection carefully: it cites the Prophet's statements about specific individuals like Imru' al-Qais and Hatim al-Ta'i being in Hell. Note the companion's question about his father.
These are meant to challenge the principle that punishment only comes after a messenger.
2
Identify the two answers
The author gives two answers. First: the hadiths are solitary (ahad) and cannot override definitive (qat'i) texts like Quran 17:15. Second: those individuals might have specific reasons known only to Allah for their punishment.
The first answer is methodological, the second is a possibility.
3
Note the exclusion of sound senses
The author adds that the condition of sound senses (salim al-hawass) is necessary for moral responsibility. A blind and deaf person is not obligated to reflect or be held accountable.
This shows that not all ahl al-fatrah are equal; some may lack the capacity for obligation.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Reflect on how this passage balances between prophetic reports and Quranic principles, using the classification of hadith (ahad vs qat'i) and allowing for hidden wisdom.
This is a key example of how kalam arguments handle apparent conflicts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: كيف هذا مع أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أخبر بأن جماعة من أهل الفترة في النار؛ كامرئ القيس وحاتم الطائي وبعض آباء الصحابة؛ فإن بعض الصحابة سأله وهو يخطب فقال: أين أبي؟ فقال: في النار. أجيب بأن أحاديثهم أحاديث آحاد وهي لا تعارض القطعي، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا كُنَّا مُعَذِّبِينَ حَتَّى نَبْعَثَ رَسُولا﴾ [الإسراء: 15]، وبأنه يجوز أن يكون تعذيب من صح تعذيبه منهم لأمر يختص به يعلمه الله تعالى ورسوله. وخرج بسليم الحواس: غيره، ولهذا قال بعض أئمة الشافعية: لو خلق الله إنساناً أعمى أصم، سقط عنه وجوب النظر والتكليف، وهو صحيح كما في «شرح المصنف».
If it is said: How is this when the Prophet (peace be upon him) informed that a group of the people of the fatrah are in the Fire, such as Imru' al-Qais and Hatim al-Ta'i and some of the fathers of the Companions; for a Companion asked him while he was delivering a sermon, 'Where is my father?' He said, 'In the Fire.' The answer is that their hadiths are solitary (ahad) hadiths and they do not contradict the definitive (qat'i), which is His statement, the Most High: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' [17:15], and that it is possible that the punishment of those whose punishment is confirmed from among them is for a matter specific to them known to Allah, the Most High, and His Messenger. And he excluded by 'sound of senses' (salim al-hawass) other than it, and for this said some of the Shafi'i imams: If Allah created a person blind and deaf, the obligation of reflection (nazar) and responsibility (taklif) would fall from him, and it is correct as in Sharh al-Musannaf.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents an objection concerning the fate of people who lived in times without prophets (ahl al-fatrah) and provides two answers. The objection cites hadiths indicating that some of them are in Hell, such as Imru' al-Qais and Hatim al-Ta'i, and some companions' fathers. The first answer is that these hadiths are solitary (ahad) and do not override the definitive (qat'i) Qur'anic verse (17:15) which states that punishment does not occur before sending a messenger. The second answer allows that those who are punished may have specific reasons known to God. Then the author adds that the condition of sound senses is necessary for obligation, so a blind and deaf person is not obliged to reflect or be responsible.

taklif

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All ahl al-fatrah are automatically punished because some hadiths mention specific individuals in Hell.
Correction: The hadiths are solitary and do not override the definitive Quranic principle; also, those individuals may have had unique circumstances.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, why do the hadiths about some ahl al-fatrah being in Hell not contradict the Quranic verse about punishment only after sending a messenger?
A) The hadiths are weak and cannot be used as evidence.
B) The hadiths are solitary (ahad) and do not override definitive (qat'i) texts.
C) The hadiths are about people who already received a messenger.
D) The Quranic verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The author defends the principle of no punishment without prophetic warning by classifying contradictory hadiths as ahad and allowing for unknown individual circumstances.

The Salvation of the Prophet's Parents and Ancestors
The author argues for the salvation of the Prophet's family and refutes a false attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the evidence for the salvation of the Prophet's parents and ancestors and why the attribution of disbelief to Abu Hanifa is false.

Core Idea
The Prophet's parents and ancestors are saved; the claim otherwise is a forgery.

This lesson examines a key issue in Sunni theology: the state of the Prophet's parents. The author argues that they are among the saved people of the fatrah and provides textual proofs. He also clarifies the identity of Azar and refutes a claim misattributed to Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of ahl al-fatrah
Realize that the people of the fatrah (interval between prophets) are considered saved, which is the premise for the Prophet's parents' salvation.
This principle is foundational for the whole argument.
2
Examine the transmitted evidences
Identify the Quranic verse and prophetic hadith that the author cites to prove the purity of the Prophet's lineage.
The hadith is said to be mutawatir, strengthening the proof.
3
Analyze the clarification and refutation
Note the explanation of Azar as Ibrahim's uncle, not father, and understand that the claim that Abu Hanifa said the Prophet's parents died in disbelief is a forgery.
This shows the author's effort to protect the Prophet's honor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه: إذا علمت أن أهل الفترة ناجون على الراجح، علمت أن أبويه صلى الله عليه وسلم ناجيان؛ لكونهما من أهل الفترة، بل جميع آبائه وأمهاته ناجون ومحكوم بإيمانهم لم يدخلهم كفر ولا رجس ولا عيب ولا شيء مما كان عليه الجاهلية؛ بأدلة نقلية؛ كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَتَقَلُّبَكَ فِي السَّاجِدِينَ﴾ [الشعراء: 219]، وقوله: لم أزل أتنقل من الأصلاب الطاهرات إلى الأرحام الزاكيات، وغير ذلك من الأحاديث البالغة مبلغ التواتر. وأما آزر فكان عم إبراهيم، وإنما دعاه بالأب لأن عادة العرب تدعو العم بالأب. وأما ما نقل عن أبي حنيفة في الفقه الأكبر من أن والدي المصطفى ماتا على الكفر، فمدسوس عليه، وحاشاه أن يقول في والدي المصطفى ذلك، وغلط ملا علي.
Note: When you know that the people of the fatrah are saved according to the most correct view, you know that his two parents (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) are saved, because they are from the people of the fatrah. Rather, all of his fathers and mothers are saved and judged to have faith (iman); no disbelief (kufr), impurity (rijs), defect ('ayb), or anything of what the Jahiliyyah was upon entered them, based on transmitted (naqli) evidences, such as His statement, the Most High: 'And your moving among those who prostrate' [26:219], and his statement (peace be upon him): 'I have never ceased to be transferred from pure loins to pure wombs,' and other hadiths that have reached the level of tawatur. As for Azar, he was the uncle of Ibrahim, and he only called him 'father' because the custom of the Arabs is to call the uncle 'father.' And as for what was transmitted from Abu Hanifa in al-Fiqh al-Akbar that the parents of the Chosen One (al-Mustafa) died upon disbelief, it is forged upon him, and far be it from him to say that about the parents of al-Mustafa, and Mulla 'Ali was mistaken.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage addresses the status of the Prophet's parents and ancestors. It begins by noting that since ahl al-fatrah are saved, the Prophet's parents, being from that period, are saved. It extends this to all his ancestors, asserting their faith and purity from any Jahiliyyah practices. It cites Quranic and hadith proofs, including the verse 'And your moving among those who prostrate' and the hadith about being transferred from pure loins to pure wombs, which are reported at the level of tawatur. Then it addresses the case of Azar, explaining he was Ibrahim's uncle, not father, as the Arabs customarily call uncles 'fathers'. Finally, it refutes the claim that Abu Hanifa held that the Prophet's parents died in disbelief, calling it a forgery, and states that Mulla 'Ali was mistaken in that attribution.

The Salvation of the Prophet's Parents
Affirms salvation of Prophet's parents and cites revival hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that the Prophet's parents are saved, and the role of the weak hadith about their revival in supporting this belief.

Core Idea
The Prophet's parents are saved; even a weak hadith can support a sound doctrine.

This passage from al-Jawharah addresses a controversial topic: the fate of the Prophet's parents. The author affirms that they are saved (nājiyān) and cites a hadith about their revival, despite its weakness, to show that God is capable of such a miracle. We'll examine the text, the hadith, and the scholarly commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's opening statement and the flow from doctrinal assertion to supporting evidence.
2
Identify the author's explicit position
The author states 'the truth with which we meet God is that his two parents are saved.' This is a clear doctrinal claim.
3
Examine the supporting hadith
The hadith from 'A'isha says the Prophet asked God to revive his parents, they believed, then died again. Note that the author acknowledges the hadith is weak but still uses it.
The poem in the passage reinforces the point: 'even if the hadith is weak, God is powerful.'
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the author combines a clear doctrinal statement, a weak hadith, a scholarly quote, and a poem to establish the position. What does this teach about how theologians use evidence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحق الذي نلقى الله عليه: أن أبويه ناجيان . على أنه قيل : إنه تعالى أحياهما حتّى آمنا به ثم أماتهما؛ لحديث ورد في ذلك: وهو ما رُوي عن عروة، عن عائشة: أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم سأل ربَّه أن يحيي له أبويه، فأحياهما، فآمنا به، ثم أماتهما. قال السهيلي: (والله قادر على كل شيء، له أن يخص نبيه بما شاء من فضله، وينعم عليه بما شاء من كرامته) انتهى. وقد أنشد بعضُهم فقال: [من الوافر] حبا الله النبيَّ مَزِيدَ فَضْلٍ عَلى فَضْل وكانَ به رَؤُوفَا فأَحْيَا أَمَّهُ وكَذَا أَبَاهُ الإيمانِ بهِ فَضْلاً مُنِيفًا فَسَلَّمْ فَالقَدِيمُ بِذَا قَدِيرٌ وَإِنْ كَانَ الحَدِيْثُ بِهِ ضَعِيفَا
The truth with which we meet God is that his two parents are saved. However, it is said: He, exalted, revived them until they believed in him, then caused them to die, due to a hadith reported about that: which is what was narrated by 'Urwa from 'A'isha: that the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, asked his Lord to revive his parents for him, so He revived them, and they believed in him, then He caused them to die. Al-Suhayli said: 'God is powerful over everything, He may single out His Prophet with whatever He wills of His bounty and bestow upon him whatever He wills of His honor.' End. And one of them recited: [al-Wafir] God favored the Prophet with additional grace upon grace, and He was merciful to him. So He revived his mother and likewise his father for faith in Him, an exalted favor. So submit: the Eternal is powerful over that, even if the hadith about it is weak.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal affirmation combined with a supporting hadith and scholarly opinions. The author begins by stating the truth upon which we meet God: that both parents of the Prophet (peace be upon him) are saved (nājiyān). He then mentions a report (qīla) that God revived them so they believed, then caused them to die again, citing a hadith from 'A'isha. This is followed by a quote from al-Suhayli emphasizing God's absolute power and His prerogative to bestow favors on His prophet. Finally, a poem by Ibn Nāṣir al-Dīn al-Dimashqī is cited, which poetically affirms the revival, saying that God gave the Prophet extra grace, and even if the hadith is weak, the Eternal One is powerful enough to do it. The author does not rely solely on the weak hadith but presents it as a possible vehicle for the truth of their salvation.

Misconception: That the hadith of revival is universally accepted as authentic and is the sole basis for the position.
Correction: The author acknowledges the hadith is weak but still uses it, showing that doctrinal issues can rely on multiple supports, including scholarly consensus and God's power.

The Benefit of Reviving the Prophet's Parents
Objection and answer about the benefit of reviving the parents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological rationale behind reviving the Prophet's parents after they already believed, and the concept of a special perfection granted to them.

Core Idea
God's actions have wise purposes beyond what we initially perceive.

This lesson addresses a natural question that arises when considering the special honor given to the Prophet's parents: if they already believed before their revival, what added benefit did the revival bring? The answer, drawn from Ibn Hajar, reveals a profound point about degrees of perfection in faith and God's generosity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the flow of the objection and answer.
Notice the concise style of the objection-and-answer format.
2
Identify the objection
The objection questions the utility of reviving the parents if they already had faith. Understand why someone might think it is unnecessary.
This objection reflects a limited view of divine wisdom.
3
Examine the answer
Ibn Hajar's answer invokes the concept of 'kamaal' (perfection) that the people of the fatra did not receive. Consider what this perfection might entail.
'Perfection' here likely refers to the explicit belief in the Prophet ﷺ after his mission.
4
Reflect on the deeper lesson
This shows that God's blessings have layers. Mere belief is one level; witnessing the Prophet and believing explicitly is a higher rank.
This also affirms that God's acts are never without purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فما فائدة إحيائهما مع كون الإيمان قد ثبت لهما؟ فالجواب كما قال العلامة المحقق ابن حجر في شرح الهمزية (ص ١٠١): إلحاقهما بكمال لم يحصل لأهل الفترة.
For if it is said: What is the benefit of reviving them while faith has already been established for them? The answer is as the investigating scholar Ibn Hajar said in his commentary on al-Hamziyya (p. 101): 'To join them with a perfection not obtained by the people of the fatra.'
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objection questions the utility of reviving the Prophet's parents after they already believed (since faith alone would suffice for salvation). The answer, drawn from Ibn Hajar al-'Asqalani, is that the revival served to attach them to a perfection (kamāl) that the people of the fatra (the interval between prophets) did not attain. This perfection may refer to experiencing explicit belief in the Prophet after his mission, as opposed to the mere natural faith of those who died before the message reached them. The answer affirms that God's acts have wise purposes beyond what is immediately apparent.

Key Terms
فائدة (benefit)
إحياء (revival)
كمال (perfection)
فترة (interval, fatra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reviving the Prophet's parents was unnecessary because they already believed and faith alone is sufficient for salvation.
Correction: The revival granted them a special perfection (kamal) not attained by those who believed during the fatra, showing that God's blessings are not limited to mere salvation but include higher degrees of honor.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The revival of the Prophet’s parents granted them a special perfection beyond mere belief, a perfection not attained by those who died in the fatra.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you willing to trust that He is adding perfection to your soul through every circumstance? Will you commit to patience and gratitude?

The Benefit of Resurrecting the Prophet's Parents
Objection and answer about resurrection benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological benefit of resurrecting the Prophet's parents despite their prior faith.

Core Idea
Resurrection grants the Prophet's parents a perfection beyond ordinary faith.

This short objection-and-answer addresses a subtle point: if the Prophet's parents already had faith, why resurrect them? The answer, from Ibn Hajar, reveals a deeper theological nuance about divine grace bestowing an elevated perfection not attainable in their natural lives.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Notice it assumes faith was already established for the Prophet's parents.
2
Understand the answer
The answer quotes Ibn Hajar: they are joined to a perfection not attained by the people of the fatra. Reflect on what 'perfection' might mean here.
The fatra refers to the period between prophets when no revelation was sent.
3
Connect to broader theology
Consider why this answer satisfies the objection. It shows that resurrection can bestow additional spiritual ranks beyond mere faith.
This aligns with the idea that God's mercy can grant extra degrees of honor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فما فائدة إحيائهما مع كون الإيمان قد ثبت لهما؟ فالجواب كما قال العلامة المحقق ابن حجر في شرح الهمزية (ص 101): (إتحاقهما بكمال لم يحصل لأهل الفترة). والكشف عند القوم غاية سبل اليقين ولا مرمى وراءه والإلهام نوع منه، وهو حجة قاطعة بشرطه وشرطه العصمة الواجبة التي لا تكون لغير نبي، فلذا كان الإلهام والرؤى المنامية في حقهم صلوات ربي عليهم وحياً يجب العمل به، أما العصمة الوهبية (الحفظ والتولي) التي تكون الخيار الخلق وصفوة الحق ، فلا تكفي؛ ولكن إن حُفّت بقرائن تشهد لهذا الكشف - وأوجبها عدم مخالفة ظاهر الشرع، وكونُ الكشف مؤكّداً له بإظهار حِكَمِهِ وآثاره _ كان هذا الكشف صحيحاً، حجة لصاحبه، وبشارة لمن صدق به، فإن سمعت بكشف مخالف لظاهر الشرع وكنت عالماً بالظاهر، فاعلم أنه تلاعب شيطان .
If it is said: What is the benefit of resurrecting them when faith has already been established for them? The answer is as the verifying scholar Ibn Hajar said in his commentary on the Hamziyya (p. 101): 'They are joined to a perfection that was not attained by the people of the Fatra.' And unveiling (kashf) according to the people is the ultimate way of certainty, and there is no goal beyond it, and inspiration (ilham) is a type of it. It is a decisive proof with its condition, and its condition is the obligatory infallibility which does not exist for other than a prophet. Therefore, inspiration and dream visions in their case (peace be upon them) are revelation that must be acted upon. As for the gifted infallibility (preservation and guardianship) that belongs to the elite of creation and the choicest of truth, it is not sufficient. But if it is accompanied by indications that testify to this unveiling—the most important of which is that it does not contradict the outward of the Law, and that the unveiling confirms the Law by manifesting its wisdoms and effects—then this unveiling is sound, a proof for its owner, and good tidings for whoever believes in it. If you hear of an unveiling that contradicts the outward of the Law and you are knowledgeable of the outward, then know that it is devilish tampering.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a definition of kashf. First, an objection is raised: if the Prophet's parents already believed, what benefit is there in resurrecting them? The answer, quoting Ibn Hajar, is that they are joined to a perfection not attained by the people of the fatra (the interval between prophets). The commentator then defines kashf (unveiling) as the highest form of certainty, a type of inspiration. He explains that it is a decisive proof only when accompanied by the condition of infallibility ('isma) which is exclusive to prophets. Thus, for non-prophets, inspiration and dreams are not authoritative unless they meet specific conditions: they must not contradict the outward meaning of the Law, and they must confirm it by revealing its wisdoms and effects. If a claimed unveiling contradicts the Law, it is to be rejected as devilish tampering.

Key Terms
إحياء الوالدين
أهل الفترة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the resurrection of the Prophet's parents is redundant because they already believed.
Correction: The passage clarifies it is not redundant; it grants them a unique perfection not possible in their earthly life.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the benefit of resurrecting the Prophet's parents?
A) To grant them faith which they lacked.
B) To join them to a perfection not attained by the people of the fatra.
C) To prove that resurrection is possible.
D) To honor the Prophet's tribe.

Key Takeaway
The resurrection of the Prophet's parents bestows a unique perfection beyond the faith they possessed in life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objection asks: 'What is the benefit of resurrecting them when faith has already been established for them?' This reveals a tendency to question God's actions when they seem redundant. Where in your own life have you doubted the wisdom of God's decrees because they seemed unnecessary? How does this lesson challenge your reliance on your own measure of benefit? Commit to trusting His hidden wisdom today.
?
The answer speaks of a 'perfection' not attained by others. If the Prophet's parents receive an elevated status through resurrection, what does that teach you about the depths of God's mercy and honour for those close to His beloved? Does this stir in you a greater love for the Prophet and a desire to be among his honoured? What one step can you take today to deepen your connection to him?
